Classification of Divine Bounties: Essential and Ornamental
The author enumerates two divisions of divine bounties: accessories of sufficiency and accessories of adornment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two categories of divine bounties according to the sages: essential bounties that meet basic needs and ornamental bounties that provide extra delight.

Core Idea
Allah's bounties are either for sufficiency or for adornment.

The wise scholars (al-ḥukamā') have classified the countless bounties Allah bestows upon His servants into two fundamental types. This classification helps us appreciate both the necessities of life and the extra comforts that beautify existence. In this passage, the author explains these two categories with concrete examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Understand the main division introduced.
Notice the author begins with a reason for the classification: 'For this reason...'
2
Identify the two categories
Look for the two divisions: 'one is of the accessories of sufficiency' and 'the other is of the accessories of adornment'. Understand their defining characteristics.
The first category fulfills the essential purpose without extra pleasure; the second contains added delight.
3
Reflect on the examples
Consider the examples given: grains like barley and corn for sufficiency; appetizing foods for adornment. Compare them to understand the distinction.
Clothing examples: wool and hair for covering vs. fine garments for adornment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا قسمت الحكماء ما ساق الله تعالى إلى عباده من أنواع نعمه قسمين: أحدهما من لواحق المرفق وهو ما يحصل به المقصود من غير أن يكون فيه زيادة لذة كالشعير والذرة، وغير ذلك من الحبوب التي يحصل بتناولها بقاء الأبدان وكالصوف والشعر اللذين يحصل بهما ما هو المقصود من اللباس من ستر العورة، ودفع أذى الحر والبرد. والآخر من لواحق الزينة وهو ما فيه زيادة لذة وراحة وراء ما يتعلق به من مصلحة البدن وقيام النهمة كالأطعمة المشهية والثياب الفاخرة
For this reason, the sages divided what Allah has brought to His servants of the kinds of His bounties into two divisions: one is of the accessories of sufficiency, which is what accomplishes the intended purpose without there being an increase of pleasure, such as barley and corn and other grains by consuming which the survival of bodies is attained, and such as wool and hair by which the intended purpose of clothing is attained, covering the private parts and repelling the harm of heat and cold. And the other is of the accessories of adornment, which is what contains an increase of pleasure and comfort beyond what pertains to it of benefit to the body and the satisfaction of appetite, such as appetizing foods and fine garments.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
The author presents a classification used by wise thinkers: bounties are either (1) those that achieve the necessary function with no extra pleasure, like basic grains and simple clothes, or (2) those that provide additional delight and comfort, like tasty foods and luxurious garments. This classification supports the argument that extra provision is not foolish.

Key Terms
نعم
مرفق
زينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all bounties are merely for necessity and have no purpose beyond survival.
Correction: The author shows there is a category for embellishment (زينة) that provides additional pleasure and comfort.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of the second type of bounty (ornamental)?
A) Barley.
B) Wool.
C) Appetizing foods.
D) Grains.

Key Takeaway
Allah's bounties include both essential necessities and ornamental delights, each serving a distinct purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you eat a humble meal of grains, do you stop to thank Allah for sustaining your body? And when you enjoy a delicious dish, do you recognize it as an extra bounty of adornment, or do you take it as a given? How can you cultivate awareness of both categories in your daily gratitude?
?
Look at the clothes you are wearing right now. Which parts serve sufficiency (covering, protection) and which serve adornment (style, comfort)? Are you more grateful for one over the other? What might that reveal about your heart?
?
If you had only the accessories of sufficiency—bare necessities—would you still be content and thankful? How does your attachment to adornment bounties affect your relationship with Allah? This week, commit to thanking Him specifically for one sufficiency bounty and one adornment bounty each day.

The Necessity of Divine Command and Prohibition
Demonstrates necessity of divine command and prohibition to avoid foolishness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine command and prohibition are necessary for a wise God, and how permitting evil or neglecting gratitude would be foolish.

Core Idea
A wise God must command good and forbid evil, else He would be foolish.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi establishes a core principle of Islamic theology: divine commands and prohibitions are not arbitrary but rationally necessary. The author argues that if God did not forbid evil, that would amount to permitting it, and permitting evil is incompatible with divine wisdom. He then extends the argument to gratitude and moral obligation, showing that without command and prohibition, God's blessings would be wasted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the forceful logical style and the author's use of conditional reasoning.
The passage begins with a premise about evil actions.
2
Identify the core premise
The author starts by stating that among human actions there is evil (qabih). He then argues that if God did not forbid it, that would be permission (ibaha), and permitting evil is foolish (safah).
Permission is defined as the removal of prohibition.
3
Examine the supporting reasoning
The author supports his argument by showing that removing obligation encourages evil behavior and gives free rein to the bestial soul. All of that is foolish, and prohibition is wisdom.
He lists consequences like plunder, bloodshed, and destruction.
4
Apply the argument to gratitude
The author then applies the same logic to gratitude: if God did not command thankfulness and forbid ingratitude, that would waste blessings, which is also foolish.
This shows the necessity of divine command and prohibition for all moral matters.
5
Synthesize and conclude
Understand that the necessity of divine command and prohibition flows from God's wisdom and the nature of good and evil. This refutes those who deny moral obligation.
The author references earlier arguments that invalidate the denial of obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جاهل، فكذا في أفعال الخلق ما هو قبيح، فلو لم ينه عنه الصانع لكان ذلك إباحة له إذ الإباحة هي زوال الحظر والأمر عن محتمل التكليف، وإباحة القبيح سفه. ومما يؤيد هذا أن في القول بإزالة التكليف والثواب والعقاب إعطاء الأمان للنفس الأمارة بالسوء عن عواقب المطالبات، وإغراء للنفس السبعية التي من دأبها القهر والغلبة على العبث والفساد والنهب والإغارة، وسفك الدماء وتخريب البلاد وحصول التفاني وهذا كله سفه وورود المنع عن ذلك كله حكمة، فمن ظن أن المنع عن سفه والإطلاق له حكمة فقد قلب القصة. ذلك يحققه أنه تعالى لو لم يأمر عباده بشكر ما أنعم عليهم ولم ينه عن كفرانه لكان فيه تضييع النعم وذا سفه، فلابد من الأمر بالشكر والنهي عن الكفران على ما سبق ذكره، وذلك هو التكليف. وقد سبق فى أول الفصل ما يوجب بطلان القول ببطلان التكليف فلا معنى للإعادة.
Ignorant. Thus, among the actions of creation there is what is evil. So if the Maker did not forbid it, that would be permission for it, since permission is the removal of prohibition and command from one subject to obligation. And permitting evil is foolish. What supports this is that the statement about removing obligation, reward, and punishment gives security to the soul that commands evil from the consequences of demands, and incites the bestial soul whose habit is domination and victory to frivolity, corruption, plunder, aggression, bloodshed, destruction of lands, and mutual annihilation. All of this is foolish, and the occurrence of prohibition against all that is wisdom. So whoever thinks that prohibition is foolish and allowing it is wisdom has reversed the matter. That is verified by the fact that if God Almighty did not command His servants to thank Him for His blessings and did not forbid ingratitude, that would entail wasting blessings, and that is foolish. So command to thank and prohibition of ingratitude are necessary, as mentioned before, and that is the obligation. And earlier at the beginning of the chapter came what necessitates the invalidity of the claim that obligation is invalid, so there is no need to repeat.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a proof demonstrating the necessity of divine command and prohibition. The author argues from the concept of foolishness: if God did not prohibit evil actions, that would be equivalent to permitting them, and permitting evil is foolish. He supports this by showing that removing moral obligation would encourage evil impulses. He further argues that if God did not command gratitude and forbid ingratitude, that would be wasting blessings, which is foolish. Thus, divine command and prohibition are necessary, and any claim that obligation is invalid is already refuted.

Key Terms
إباحة
حظر
تكليف
سفه
حكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's non-prohibition of evil implies approval.
Correction: The author clarifies it implies permission, which is foolish for a wise deity.

Checkpoint
What would it imply if God did not prohibit evil actions according to the author?
A) It would be a sign of wisdom.
B) It would be permission for evil, which is foolish.
C) It would show that evil is not real.
D) It would mean God is not involved.

Key Takeaway
Divine command and prohibition are necessary expressions of God's wisdom; permitting evil or leaving blessings unrecognized would be foolish.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ignore a clear command of God, do you recognize that you are essentially permitting evil in your own life? How does exposing that permission affect your next decision?
?
The passage says removing obligation gives security to the soul commanding evil. Where in your own heart do you feel that security because you haven't been confronted? What would it cost you to remove that security today?
?
If you believe God's wisdom requires prohibition of evil, why do you still argue with His commands when they inconvenience you? How does that expose a subtle belief that you know better?

Denying Miracles Attributes Incapacity to Allah
Sanusi argues against miracle deniers using the example of fire's properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying miracles implies attributing incapacity and compulsion to Allah, and how this contradicts the principle of divine omnipotence.

Core Idea
Denying miracles is denying Allah's absolute power over creation.

Sanusi continues his argument against those who reject miracles. He shows that if Allah cannot alter the properties of fire, then He is not truly omnipotent. The lesson uses the example of fire to illustrate that a voluntary agent must be able to do the opposite of what He does. This leads to the conclusion that denying miracles is a form of ignorance about Allah's power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the logical flow from the example of fire to the accusation of attributing incapacity.
The passage is truncated at the end; focus on the argument presented.
2
Identify the key concepts
Look for the terms ‘ajz (incapacity), idtirar (compulsion), and ikhtiyar (volition). Understand how the author contrasts necessary action with voluntary action.
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Analyze the analogy
Consider the analogy from creating the world from nothing to bringing forth a camel from stone. Why does the author use this comparison?
This reinforces the point that Allah's power is not limited by natural laws.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم : أنكم ساعدتمونا على أن جميع ما وجد في العالم مخلوق الله تعالى عرضا كان ذلك أو جوهرًا، وأنه تعالى موصوف بالقدرة على ما أراد إيجاده م إن النار جسم خلقه الله تعالى وخلق فيه الحرارة واليبوسة، فمن زعم أنه لا يقدر على أن يخلق هو بدل الحرارة فيها البرودة وبدل اليبوسة الرطوبة، فقد وصف الله تعالى بالعجز، وجعل أفعاله ضرورية لا اختيارية، إذ المختار والفعل كان سبيل من فعل ضد ما فعله إذ هو بذلك يمتاز عن المضطر، ووصف الله تعالى بالعجز والاضطرار جهل فاحش وكذا الله تعالى لما كان قادرًا على اختراع العالم لا عن أصل، لماذا لا يوصف بالقدرة على إخراج الناقة من الحجر، وإخراج الماء من بين
Then we say to them: You have agreed with us that everything found in the world is created by Allah Almighty, whether it is an accident or a substance, and that He is described with power over what He wills to bring into existence. [؟] Indeed, fire is a body created by Allah Almighty, and He created in it heat and dryness. Whoever claims that He cannot create in place of heat coldness, and in place of dryness moisture, has described Allah Almighty with incapacity (al-`ajz), and has made His actions necessary (daruriyya) not voluntary (ikhtiyariyya). For the voluntary agent, the way of action is that he is capable of the opposite of what he does, because thereby he is distinguished from the compelled. Describing Allah Almighty with incapacity and compulsion (idtirar) is gross ignorance. Likewise, since Allah Almighty is able to invent the world without an origin, why is He not described with the power to bring forth the she-camel from stone, and to bring forth water from between...
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument against those who deny miracles. The author begins by noting that the opponents agree that everything in the world, whether accident or substance, is created by Allah and that Allah has power over what He wills. Using the example of fire, which Allah created with heat and dryness, the author argues that if someone claims Allah cannot create cold instead of heat and moisture instead of dryness, then they are attributing incapacity (al-`ajz) to Allah and making His actions necessary rather than voluntary. A voluntary agent is distinguished by the ability to do the opposite of what he does; denying this implies compulsion (idtirar). The author condemns this as gross ignorance. Then, drawing an analogy, if Allah can create the entire world from nothing, why can He not bring forth a she-camel from stone or water from... (the argument is cut off at the end of the page). The argument thus challenges the deniers by appealing to the principle of divine omnipotence, arguing that denying specific miracles entails a denial of Allah's absolute power.

Key Terms
عجز
اضطرار
اختيار
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles break natural laws and are therefore impossible, even for Allah.
Correction: The author shows that denying miracles implies attributing incapacity to Allah, as it limits His power to act voluntarily over His creation.

Checkpoint
What does the author accuse the deniers of miracles of attributing to Allah?
A) Omnipotence (qudra)
B) Incapacity (ajz)
C) Wisdom (hikma)
D) Mercy (rahma)

Key Takeaway
Denying miracles is not just rejecting a supernatural event; it is attributing incapacity and compulsion to Allah, contradicting His absolute power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about miracles like the she-camel from stone, do you ever find yourself doubting that Allah could do such a thing? What does that doubt reveal about your belief in Allah's absolute power over creation? How will you actively fight that doubt today?
?
The author says that denying the possibility of miracles attributes incapacity (عجز) to Allah. Are there areas in your life where you act as if Allah is incapable of changing your circumstances—perhaps a difficult relationship, a sin you feel trapped in, or a dream that seems impossible? What would it look like to truly believe that nothing is too hard for Him, and what step can you take to live that belief this week?
?
If Allah's actions were necessary and not voluntary, He would be like a machine, bound by His own creation. How does the doctrine of Allah's perfect will and choice affect your trust in His wisdom and mercy? Do you live as if His actions are arbitrary or as if they are perfectly wise? How can you cultivate a deeper trust in His voluntary, wise decisions in your life right now?

God's Absolute Power Over Nature
Nasafi argues that denying God's power over nature implies weakness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying God's ability to change the properties of natural objects implies attributing incapacity and compulsion to Him.

Core Idea
God's power over nature is absolute; limiting it makes Him weak.

Nasafi begins by establishing common ground with the naturalists: they agree that all things are created by God. He then uses the example of fire to argue that if God cannot change its inherent properties—replacing heat with cold or dryness with moisture—then one would be describing God as incapable and His actions as compelled rather than voluntary. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for divine omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice how Nasafi first reminds the naturalists of their agreement before presenting his argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the common ground
Nasafi states that the naturalists agree that everything in the world is created by God and that He has power over what He wills. This is the starting point.
3
Examine the fire example
Nasafi uses the example of fire, which God created with heat and dryness. If someone claims God cannot create coldness instead of heat, they are attributing incapacity to God.
The key is that God created fire and its properties, so He can change them.
4
Understand voluntary vs. compelled action
Nasafi explains that a voluntary agent can do the opposite of what he does; a compelled agent cannot. Denying God's power to change nature makes His actions compelled, which is gross ignorance.
This distinction is central to the argument.
5
See the extension to miracles
Nasafi argues that since God created the world from nothing, He can certainly perform miracles like bringing a she-camel from a rock or giving life to a staff.
This reinforces God's absolute power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم : أنكم ساعدتمونا على أن جميع ما وجد في العالم مخلوق الله تعالى عرضا كان ذلك أو جوهرًا، وأنه تعالى موصوف بالقدرة على ما أراد إيجاده إن النار جسم خلقه الله تعالى وخلق فيه الحرارة واليبوسة، فمن زعم أنه لا يقدر على أن يخلق هو بدل الحرارة فيها البرودة وبدل اليبوسة الرطوبة، فقد وصف الله تعالى بالعجز، وجعل أفعاله ضرورية لا اختيارية، إذ المختار والفعل كان سبيل من فعل ضد ما فعله إذ هو بذلك يمتاز عن المضطر، ووصف الله تعالى بالعجز والاضطرار جهل فاحش وكذا الله تعالى لما كان قادرًا على اختراع العالم لا عن أصل، لماذا لا يوصف بالقدرة على إخراج الناقة من الحجر، وإخراج الماء من بين الأصابع، وإيجاد الحياة في العصا، وتحويل أجزاء الخشب لحما وغير ذلك.
Then we say to them: You have agreed with us that everything found in the world is created by God, whether that is an accident or a substance, and that He is described with power over what He wills to bring into existence. Indeed, fire is a body created by God, and He created in it heat and dryness. Whoever claims that He is not able to create in it coldness instead of heat and moisture instead of dryness has described God with incapacity, and made His actions necessary, not voluntary. For the voluntary agent is one whose way is to do the opposite of what he does, for by that he is distinguished from the compelled. Describing God with incapacity and compulsion is gross ignorance. Similarly, since God is able to invent the world without any preexisting matter, why should He not be described with the power to bring out a she-camel from a rock, bring out water from between fingers, give life to a staff, and transform parts of wood into flesh, and other such things?
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a direct argument addressing the naturalists. Nasafi first establishes a common ground: the naturalists agree that all things in the world (both accidents and substances) are created by God and that God has power over what He wills to create. He then uses the example of fire: God created fire and its properties of heat and dryness. If someone claims that God cannot replace heat with cold or dryness with moisture in fire, they are attributing incapacity (ʿajz) to God and making His actions necessary (ḍarūrī) rather than voluntary (ikhtiyārī). Nasafi explains that a voluntary agent is distinguished by being able to do the opposite of what he does; a compelled agent cannot. Therefore, attributing incapacity and compulsion to God is gross ignorance. He then extends the argument: since God created the entire world from nothing, He surely has the power to perform specific miracles like bringing a she-camel from stone, water from fingers, life to a staff, and transforming wood into flesh.

Key Terms
قدرة
عجز
اختيار
اضطرار
قلب الطبائع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God cannot change the natural properties He has created in objects.
Correction: Nasafi argues that since God created those properties, He has the power to change them; denying that implies incapacity.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi say about someone who claims that God cannot change the properties of fire (e.g., replace heat with cold)?
A) They affirm God's power
B) They describe God with incapacity and compulsion
C) They are correct because nature is fixed
D) They deny God's existence

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's argument establishes that denying God's power over natural properties is tantamount to denying His omnipotence and attributing weakness to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems hopeless or a habit you think is unchangeable, do you truly believe that God has the power to transform it instantly? What does your level of reliance on God's power reveal about your understanding of His absolute ability to change nature? This week, identify one area where you have been acting as if God is powerless, and consciously ask Him to intervene in that specific matter.
?
Read the list of miracles again: water from fingers, life from a staff, a camel from a rock. Do you consider these events as more difficult for God than your own daily struggles? If not, why do you sometimes pray as if God can only handle small things? Repent today for limiting God's power in your heart and commit to asking for something you previously thought was impossible.
?
Nasafi says describing God with incapacity is 'gross ignorance.' In your life, where have you subtly attributed autonomy to nature (e.g., 'that's just the way it is') rather than seeing God as directly controlling every detail? How can you start thanking God for every small change in your life, acknowledging His immediate involvement?

Divine Attributes
beginner
5 min
Naturalists' Own Doctrine Supports Miracles
Nasafi argues that naturalists' own doctrine supports miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the naturalists' own admission that substances differ by accidents to argue that they cannot rationally deny God's power to transform natures.

Core Idea
The naturalists' own theory of substance and accident actually proves God's power over nature.

Nasafi delivers a clever refutation by turning the naturalists' own beliefs against them. He points out that naturalists themselves admit that all substances are fundamentally one and differ only by accidents, and they even recognize that fire can become water through change of qualities. If they accept such natural changes, they have no rational basis to deny that God can transform natures at will. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Read the first part where Nasafi connects denial of transformation of natures to taʿṭīl (suspension of attributes). This sets up the refutation.
Taʿṭīl is a key term meaning stripping God of His attributes.
2
Examine the naturalists' own claim
Identify what naturalists believe: all substances are one kind, differences come from accidents, and fire can turn into water.
This is the core of Nasafi's argument: he uses their own doctrine.
3
See the conclusion
Reflect on Nasafi's rhetorical question: if they accept such changes naturally, why deny God's power to do the same?
Nasafi calls their denial shamelessness and foolishness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا كان إنكار قلب الطبائع نتيجة القول بالتعطيل، وفيما سبق قيل: هذا من الدلائل على إثبات القدرة الكاملة لله تعالى ما يوجب بطلان قول هؤلاء الملحدين. والذي يؤيد بطلان هذه الشبهة: أن من قول جميع الطبيعيين أو الجمهور الأعظم منهم أن جواهر العالم كلها جنس واحد واختلافها بما يوجد فيها من الأعراض، وأن جوهر النار (قد ينقلب ماء) لتبدل كيفيتي الحرارة واليبوسة بالبرودة والرطوبة. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك كيف ينكر جواز قلب الطبائع ويخرج من مقدور الله تعالى لولا الوقاحة والحماقة.
So if the denial of the transformation of natures is a result of the doctrine of taʿṭīl (suspension of attributes), and it was said previously: this is among the proofs for establishing the complete power of God, which necessitates the invalidity of the statement of these atheists. What supports the invalidity of this specious argument is that according to all naturalists, or the vast majority of them, the substances of the world are all one kind, and their difference is due to the accidents found in them, and that the substance of fire (may turn into water) due to the change of the qualities of heat and dryness into coldness and moisture. If this is the case, how can one deny the permissibility of the transformation of natures and remove it from God's power, were it not for shamelessness and foolishness.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage provides an additional support for the refutation. Nasafi first notes that denying the transformation of natures (inqilāb al-ṭabāʾiʿ) stems from the doctrine of taʿṭīl (suspending God's attributes or actions). He then mentions that this argument is among the proofs of God's complete power. The main supporting point is that the naturalists themselves, or at least the majority of them, hold that all substances (jawāhir) in the world are of one genus (jins wāḥid) and that their differences arise from the accidents (aʿrāḍ) present in them. They even admit that the substance of fire can turn into water when the qualities of heat and dryness change to coldness and moisture. Nasafi uses this against them: if they accept such transformations in nature through natural processes, they cannot rationally deny that God can bring about such changes by His power. To do so would be mere obstinacy.

Key Terms
تعطيل
جوهر
عرض
قلب الطبائع
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Naturalists completely deny all change in nature.
Correction: Nasafi shows that naturalists themselves accept change of accidents and even transformation of fire into water, so they cannot consistently deny God's power over nature.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what do most naturalists believe about the substances of the world?
A) They are many different kinds
B) They are all one kind and differ by accidents
C) They are created by God directly
D) They are eternal

Key Takeaway
Nasafi turns the naturalists' own theory of substance and accident into a proof of God's power to transform natures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that naturalists' own theory proves God's power. But do you truly live as if God has complete power over your own nature—your habits, weaknesses, and character? Where have you been acting as if change is impossible? Identify one area and commit to relying on God's qudra today.
?
Nasafi points out that denying God's power stems from shamelessness and foolishness. Where in your heart do you find a similar reluctance to submit to God's absolute power? What would it take for you to fully embrace that nothing is beyond His ability—including transforming your deepest flaws?
?
The naturalists accepted that fire can become water. Do you accept that God can change your hardened heart to softness, your ignorance to knowledge, your sin to repentance? What is one concrete step you will take this week to actively trust in God's power to transform you?

Refutation of Sarefah via Dilemma
Nasafi refutes the doctrine of sarefah by posing a logical dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses a logical dilemma to refute the doctrine of sarefah, exposing its internal contradictions and incompatibility with the principle of al-ashlah.

Core Idea
The Sarefah doctrine collapses under its own logic: either God compels or He is inconsistent.

Imam Nasafi dismantles the claim that God simply turned people's minds away from challenging the Quran. He poses a sharp dilemma: if this turning was by human choice, then God interferes in voluntary acts (which the opponent denies); if by compulsion, then God overrides human freedom (which the opponent also rejects). Then he weaponizes the opponent's own principle of al-ashlah (God must do what is best) to show the doctrine leads to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the logical flow become clear.
The passage uses a dialogue format; notice the question and the two possible answers.
2
Identify the two branches of the first dilemma
Find where Nasafi asks whether the turning away is by the slave's choice or by compulsion. Understand why each option is deemed impossible by the opponent.
The opponent must reject both, so the original claim becomes untenable.
3
Follow the second dilemma using al-ashlah
See how Nasafi then pivots to the principle that God must do what is best. He asks: if God can turn away, why does He not prevent disbelief?
This forces the opponent into another contradiction.
4
Reflect on the effectiveness of the refutation
Consider why this argument is powerful: it uses the opponent's own commitments to refute them, leaving no escape.
The dilemma shows that sarefah leads to either denial of divine power or violation of God's justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له : ما معنى صرف هممهم عن المعارضة ؟ أفعل ذلك باختيار من العبد أم لا باختياره؟ فإن قال : فعل ذلك باختيار من العبد، فهذا منه إثبات التصرف لله تعالى في الأفعال الاختيارية للعباد وتعليق قدرته بها، وهذا عنده محال، وإن فعل باضطرار من العبد، فقد أثبت الله تعالى منع قدرة العبد عما يختار من الأفعال، وهذا عنده محال ، ثم لو خالف أهل نحلته وجوز ذلك قيل له: إذا كان ذلك جائزا فلم لا يصرف همة الكافر عن الكفر وهمة العاصي عن المعصية لئلا يقع في ذلك إذ هو أصلح له، والله تعالى يجب عليه فعل الأصلح عندك؟ فإذا لم يفعل ذلك فأنت بين طرفي نقيض فإما أن تقول : لم يفعل لأنه لم يقدر وهذا يبطل القول بالصرفة، وإما أن تقول: إنه وإن قدر لم يفعل ذلك في حق الكافر والعاصي، وفيه بطلان قولك بالأصلح.
Then it is said to him: What is the meaning of turning away their efforts from opposition? Was that done by the slave's choice or not by his choice? If he says: It was done by the slave's choice, then this is from him an affirmation of God's disposal in the voluntary acts of the slaves and the attachment of His power to them, and this is for him impossible. If he says: It was done by compulsion of the slave, then he has affirmed that God prevents the slave's power over what he chooses of acts, and this is for him impossible. Then if he contradicts the people of his persuasion and permits that, it is said to him: If that is permissible, then why does He not turn away the zeal of the disbeliever from disbelief and the zeal of the sinner from sin so that they do not fall into that, since that is more optimal for him? And God is obligated to do the optimal according to you. So if He does not do that, you are between two contradictory positions: either you say He did not do it because He was not able, and this invalidates the claim of the 'sarfah' (turning away), or you say: although He was able, He did not do it with respect to the disbeliever and the sinner, and in this is the invalidation of your claim about the optimal.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage refutes the sarefah doctrine, which claims God turned people's minds away from opposing the Quran. Nasafi first poses a dilemma: is this turning away by the slave's choice or by compulsion? If by choice, it contradicts the opponent's view that God does not intervene in voluntary acts. If by compulsion, it denies human freedom, which the opponent also rejects. Then, assuming the opponent might still allow it, Nasafi uses the opponent's own principle of al-ashlah (God must do what is best) to ask why God does not turn away disbelievers from disbelief, which would be better for them. The opponent cannot answer: if he says God cannot, that denies sarefah; if he says God can but does not, that violates the principle of al-ashlah. Thus the doctrine is refuted.

Key Terms
صرفة
اختيار
اضطرار
الأصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the sarefah doctrine is a valid explanation for the Quran's inimitability because God simply prevented people from challenging it.
Correction: Nasafi shows that sarefah leads to either denying God's intervention in voluntary acts (by choice) or denying human freedom (by compulsion), and then forces a contradiction with the principle of al-ashlah, making the doctrine untenable.

Checkpoint
What is the second dilemma that Nasafi presents after assuming the opponent might permit sarefah?
A) God's inability to prevent disbelief
B) The contradiction between sarefah and the principle of al-ashlah
C) The impossibility of divine intervention in human acts
D) The role of human free will in opposition to the Quran

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes sarefah by showing it leads to contradictions about divine intervention and God's obligation to do what is best.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to argue that God should prevent evil in the world, what does Nasafi's dilemma reveal about the implications of such a claim? How does this challenge your own assumptions about God's obligations?
?
The argument shows that claiming God must always do what is optimal leads to contradictions. How do you reconcile your understanding of God's wisdom with the existence of evil and disbelief? Does this lesson push you to accept that God's actions are not bound by human notions of 'optimal'?
?
Reflect on your own life: Are there times when you wished God would have prevented you from sinning? What does this lesson teach you about the necessity of free will and personal responsibility? Write a commitment to rely on God's wisdom even when His decrees seem hard.

The Dilemma of Sarfa and Al-Aslah
Dialectical refutation of sarfa via dilemma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the doctrine of sarfa (diverting intentions) leads to a logical contradiction with the principle that God must do what is most beneficial (al-aslah).

Core Idea
Sarfa forces a dilemma: either God is powerless or He fails to do what is best.

This passage presents a powerful theological dilemma. If God can turn people away from evil, why does He not turn all disbelievers and sinners? The Mu'tazilite principle of al-aslah (God must do the most beneficial) makes this a devastating objection against the doctrine of sarfa. Let's trace the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two horns of the dilemma
The author asks whether the sarfa happens by the slave's choice or by compulsion, showing both are problematic for the opponent.
Notice that each horn contradicts a key Mu'tazilite tenet.
2
Connect sarfa to al-aslah
If the opponent concedes sarfa is possible, then ask why God does not apply it universally to prevent evil, since it would be more beneficial.
This exploits the opponent's own commitment to al-aslah.
3
Recognize the contradiction
Either God is unable to apply sarfa (contradicting its possibility) or He is able but chooses not to (contradicting the duty to do what is best).
Thus sarfa and al-aslah cannot both be true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له : ما معنى صرف هممهم عن المعارضة ؟ أفعل ذلك باختيار من العبد أم لا باختياره؟ فإن قال : فعل ذلك باختيار من العبد، فهذا منه إثبات التصرف (۲) لله تعالى في الأفعال الاختيارية للعباد وتعليق قدرته بها، وهذا عنده محال، وإن فعل باضطرار من العبد، فقد أثبت الله تعالى منع قدرة العبد عما يختار من الأفعال، وهذا عنده محال ، ثم لو خالف أهل نحلته وجوز ذلك قيل له: إذا كان ذلك جائزا فلم لا يصرف همة الكافر عن الكفر وهمة العاصي عن المعصية لئلا يقع في ذلك إذ هو أصلح له، والله تعالى يجب عليه فعل الأصلح عندك (۳)؟ فإذا لم يفعل ذلك فأنت بين طرفي نقيض فإما أن تقول : لم يفعل لأنه لم يقدر وهذا يبطل القول بالصرفة، وإما أن تقول: إنه وإن قدر لم يفعل ذلك في حق الكافر والعاصي، وفيه بطلان قولك بالأصلح.
Then it is said to him: What is the meaning of diverting their intentions from opposition? Did He do that by the slave's choice or not by his choice? If he says: He did that by the slave's choice, then this from him is affirming God's disposal in the voluntary acts of the slaves and attaching His power to them, and this is impossible in his view. If he says: He did it by compulsion from the slave, then he has affirmed that God prevents the slave's power from what he chooses of acts, and this is impossible in his view. Then if he deviates from the people of his sect and allows that, it is said to him: Since that is permissible, why does He not divert the intention of the disbeliever from disbelief and the intention of the sinner from sin so that he does not fall into it, since it is more beneficial for him, and God is required to do the most beneficial according to you? So if He does not do that, you are between two contradictory extremes: either you say that He did not do it because He was not able, and this invalidates the doctrine of sarfa; or you say that although He was able, He did not do that regarding the disbeliever and the sinner, and in this lies the invalidation of your doctrine of al-aslah.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of the doctrine of sarfa (the idea that God turned the Arabs away from producing a counter to the Quran). The author poses a dilemma: if the turning away (sarfa) happened by the slave's choice, then God is intervening in voluntary acts, which the opponent denies; if by compulsion, then God prevents the slave's power, again denied by the opponent. Then if the opponent concedes that sarfa is possible, the author asks why God does not turn away all disbelievers and sinners from evil since that would be more beneficial (aslah) for them—a principle the opponent affirms. Since God does not do so, the opponent is caught: either God is unable (invalidating sarfa) or He is able but does not do it (invalidating al-aslah). Thus sarfa cannot stand.

Key Terms
صرفة
أصلح
اختيار
اضطرار
تصرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sarfa is a consistent explanation for the Quran's inimitability without violating divine justice.
Correction: Sarfa leads to a dilemma: either it undermines human agency or it forces God to be inconsistent with the principle of al-aslah.

Checkpoint
What is the main logical problem the author presents against the doctrine of sarfa?
A) It denies human free will entirely.
B) It leads to a contradiction with the opponent's doctrine of al-aslah (God must do the most beneficial).
C) It implies that the Quran is not a miracle.
D) It requires that God be unable to prevent evil.

Key Takeaway
The doctrine of sarfa cannot be consistently maintained without contradicting either divine power or divine justice under the Mu'tazilite framework.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone persisting in disbelief or sin, do you ever wonder why God does not simply turn their heart? How does this passage challenge your assumptions about divine power and human responsibility? Will you now examine whether you have been relying on a comfortable theology that avoids such tensions?
?
This argument exposes hidden contradictions in a theological system. Where in your own understanding of God’s attributes—such as His power, justice, and mercy—might there be unresolved tensions you have never faced? Are you willing to confront them, even if it unsettles your current beliefs?
?
If God is able to turn hearts but does not always do so, what does that mean for your own need for His guidance? Do you take His guidance for granted, or do you actively seek it with the awareness that He could leave you to your own devices? Will you pray today with true humility, acknowledging that your own faith is sustained only by His will?

Three Aspects of the Qur'an's Inimitability
The author lists the three aspects of Qur'anic inimitability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three aspects of the Qur'an's miraculous nature as explained by Imam al-Nasafi.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's miracle is not just style but content: future knowledge, perfect wisdom, and immense knowledge in a small volume.

Imam al-Nasafi presents three distinct aspects of the Qur'an's i'jaz (inimitability) that go beyond linguistic eloquence. These aspects highlight the divine origin of the Qur'an through its comprehensive knowledge, profound wisdom, and compactness of content. Let's explore each one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then its English translation. Notice how the author structures the three aspects.
The passage is a clear enumeration—look for 'أحدها', 'والثاني', 'والثالث'.
2
Identify the first aspect
The first aspect is knowledge of future events until the Day of Resurrection. This is knowledge of the unseen (ghayb) that only Allah possesses.
3
Examine the second aspect
The second aspect is the wisdom (hikmah) and benefit (maslahah) in all rulings. The author argues that an unlettered man could not have derived such comprehensive wisdom without divine revelation.
This aspect covers acts of worship, transactions, inheritances, and social interactions for all times.
4
Understand the third aspect
The third aspect is that the Qur'an, in a relatively small volume, contains knowledge that, if expanded, would fill thousands of volumes. This indicates its divine source.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how these three aspects collectively prove that the Qur'an is from Allah and beyond human capacity.
The passage ends with a prayer for divine success.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكان فيه الإعجاز من أوجه ثلاثة: أحدها العلم بما يكون في المستقبل إلى قيام الساعة، وهو علم الغيب لا يعلمه إلا الله تعالى. والثاني: العلم بوجه الحكمة والمصلحة في كل ذلك، وليس في طباع البشر أن يعلم رجل أمي لم ينفق عمره في إحراز العلوم واكتساب الآداب والحكم وجهة الحكمة والصواب والمصلحة في حوادث أسبوع واحد لإنسان واحد من جنس واحد فضلاً عن جميع أجناس الحوادث الواقعة في العبادات، وأنواع المعاملات والمواريث والمعاشرات لجميع العالم إلى آباد الدهر، فعلم أن ذلك ورد من عند علام الغيوب الذي لا يعزب عنه شيء ولا يخفى عليه خافية. والثالث: أنه ليس في مقدور البشر أن يودع في واحد منهم في مقدار دفتر من الكتاب ما يربو من العلوم عند التفتيش والإيضاح والبسط على ألف دفتر وزيادة، فدل أن ذلك بالقادر الذي لا يصعب عليه أمر ولا يعجزه شيء، والله الموفق.
Then the inimitability in it is from three aspects: First: knowledge of what will happen in the future until the Day of Resurrection, which is knowledge of the unseen that none knows except Allah Almighty. Second: knowledge of the aspect of wisdom and benefit in all of that; and it is not in the nature of humans that an illiterate man who did not spend his life acquiring sciences and gaining manners and wisdom should know the aspect of wisdom, correctness, and benefit in the events of a single week for a single human of a single species, let alone all species of events occurring in acts of worship, and types of transactions, inheritances, and social interactions for all the world until the end of time. Thus it is known that this came from the Knower of the unseen from whom nothing is hidden and no secret is concealed. Third: that it is not within the capacity of humans to place in one of them, in the size of a notebook of the Book, that which, upon investigation, clarification, and expansion, over a thousand notebooks and more. So it indicates that this is from the Powerful One for whom nothing is difficult and nothing is impossible. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage defines the three aspects of the Qur'an's miraculous nature (i'jaz). The author lists them in order. The first aspect is the Qur'an's comprehensive knowledge of future events until the Day of Resurrection—this is knowledge of the unseen that only Allah possesses. The second aspect is the profound wisdom and benefit contained in the Qur'anic rulings for all types of acts of worship, transactions, inheritances, and social interactions for all people for all time. The author emphasizes that it is impossible for an unlettered man (the Prophet) to have derived this knowledge without divine revelation. The third aspect is the density of knowledge in a relatively small volume—the Qur'an contains in its volume what would fill thousands of volumes if expanded. This shows it is from the omnipotent Allah. The passage concludes with a prayer for divine assistance.

Key Terms
إعجاز (i'jaz)
علم الغيب (knowledge of the unseen)
حكمة (wisdom)
مصلحة (benefit)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The i'jaz of the Qur'an is only about its linguistic eloquence and style.
Correction: While eloquence is part of i'jaz, this passage shows that the miracle also includes content: knowledge of the unseen, comprehensive wisdom, and density of knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the third aspect of the Qur'an's i'jaz according to the author?
A) Knowledge of future events
B) Wisdom in all rulings
C) That a small volume contains knowledge that would fill thousands of volumes upon expansion
D) Inimitability in style

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an's inimitability consists of three aspects: knowledge of the unseen, perfect wisdom, and a wealth of knowledge in a compact form—all proving its divine origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The second aspect says the Prophet, an illiterate man, brought wisdom covering all of life. What does that mean for you when you turn to the Qur'an for guidance? Do you approach it as a source of perfect wisdom for your life, or do you sometimes doubt its applicability? What would change if you treated every command as part of that divine wisdom?
?
The third aspect speaks of immense knowledge in a small volume. Have you ever experienced the depth of a single verse? If not, what might be blocking you? Commit today to reading one verse with the expectation that it holds more than you can imagine.
?
The first aspect mentions knowledge of the unseen, including your own death and the Last Day. Do you live with the reality that Allah alone knows your future? How does that shape your actions today? What one step can you take this week to align your life with that unseen truth?

Two Qur'anic Proofs for Resurrection: Knowledge and Power
Imam Nasafi presents two Qur'anic proofs: God's encompassing knowledge and His power manifest in creating fire from green wood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi uses two Qur'anic verses—one on God's comprehensive knowledge and one on His power to create fire from green wood—to demonstrate the logical possibility of resurrection.

Core Idea
If God can create fire from green wood, resurrecting the dead is even easier.

Imam Nasafi presents two arguments from the Qur'an to show that resurrection is not only possible but easy for God. The first argument appeals to God's all-encompassing knowledge, which is necessary for a precise act like resurrection. The second argument draws on the miracle of bringing fire from a water-filled tree, highlighting God's boundless power and subtle arrangement. These verses together build a compelling case for the return of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the two parts: the knowledge argument and the power argument.
2
Identify the two Qur'anic proofs
Find the two verses cited. Understand how each supports the conclusion about resurrection.
3
Analyse the analogy of fire from green wood
Consider the opposition between water and fire. Why is this a powerful analogy for resurrection?
The passage argues that if God can overcome such opposition, resurrection is even easier.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
How does the author connect these arguments to the possibility of resurrection? What do they say about God's attributes?
The passage shows God's knowledge, power, will, and arrangement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بقوله تعالى: (وَهُوَ بِكُلِّ خَلْقٍ عَلِيمُ ) بين أن الإعادة وإن كان فعلاً محكماً لا تتأتى إلا من عالم فالله تعالى عالم بعلم يحيط بالمعلومات أجمع، ثم بقوله: ﴿ الَّذِي جَعَلَ لَكُم مِّنَ الشَّجَرِ الْأَخْضَرِ نَارًا ) بين كمال قدرته وعجيب تدبيره أنه جعل من الشجر الأخضر الذي رويت أصوله وفروعه وغصونه من الماء إذ جعل من الأخضر بدون الماء نارا مع تضاد في كيفيتهما، إذ الماء بارد رطب والنار حارة يابسة، ولم يصعب عليه ذلك لكمال قدرته ونفاذ مشيئته وصائب تدبيره ولطف تقديره، وأعلم أن من له هذه القدرة والمشيئة وهذا التدبير والتقدير لا يبعد عنه إيجاد الحياة في العظام الرميمة والأجساد البالية، إذ ليس فيها ما يضاد الحياة، ولو كان فيها ذلك لكان قادرا على إعدام المضاد وإيجاد الحياة وذلك أقرب في تقدير عقولنا من جعل النار من الشجر الأخضر مع بقاء خضرته ووجود ما فيه من الماء
Then by His saying, the Exalted: "And He is of every creation Knowing," He explained that the return, even if it is a precise act, is not possible except from a knower. So Allah the Exalted is Knowing with a knowledge that encompasses all known things. Then by His saying: "He who made for you from the green tree fire," He explained the perfection of His power and wondrous arrangement—that He made from the green tree whose roots, branches, and twigs were watered (by water) fire, while He made from the green (tree) without water fire, despite the opposition in their qualities—for water is cold and moist, and fire is hot and dry. And that was not difficult for Him due to the perfection of His power, the penetrability of His will, the correctness of His arrangement, and the subtlety of His determination. And He made known that whoever has this power and will and this arrangement and determination, it is not far from Him to bring life to decayed bones and worn-out bodies, for there is nothing in them that opposes life. And if there were in them that, He would be able to remove the opposer and bring life, and that is closer in the estimation of our intellects than making fire from green tree while its greenness remains and the existence of what is in it of water.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a combined proof from two Qur'anic verses. First, the verse about God's comprehensive knowledge: resurrection is a precise act that requires full knowledge of the original creation, and God possesses that. Second, the analogy of fire from green wood: God produces fire from a tree full of water, which is a greater marvel because of the opposition between water and fire. The author argues that if God can do that, then reviving the dead is even more fitting since there is no inherent opposition to life in dead bones. The passage concludes that such power makes resurrection easy.

Key Terms
علم
قدرة
مشيئة
تدبير
تقدير
مضاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Resurrection is more difficult than the miracle of creating fire from green wood.
Correction: The passage argues that resurrection is actually easier because dead bones contain no element that opposes life, unlike the water that opposes fire in the tree.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the Qur'an use to demonstrate God's power to resurrect?
A) Creating the heavens and earth
B) Bringing fire from green wood
C) Creating man from clay
D) Parting the sea

Key Takeaway
God's all-encompassing knowledge and His power to create fire from green wood demonstrate that resurrection is not only possible but easy for Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems impossible—like bringing life from decay—do you truly believe God's power is not bound by the obstacles you see? What would it look like for you to surrender that impossibility to Him today?
?
The green tree contains water, yet God brings fire from it. Is there an area of your life where you are clinging to 'water' (your perceived weakness) and failing to trust that God can produce 'fire' (strength)? What is that area, and what is one step you can take to hand it over to His power?
?
If resurrection is 'easier' than making fire from green wood, why do you still doubt God's ability to revive dead hopes, relationships, or faith in your own heart? What specific dead thing do you need to ask Him to resurrect right now?

The Ummah's Constant Devotion to Tawhid
Author describes the Ummah's universal practice of tawhid recitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ummah's constant recitation of tawhid serves as a sign of their commitment and is commanded by Allah.

Core Idea
The Ummah fills the earth with tawhid, day and night.

In this passage, the author highlights a remarkable characteristic of the Muslim Ummah: their continuous recitation of the word of tawhid (kalimat al-ikhlāṣ) in all circumstances, unlike other religious communities. This practice is rooted in Allah's command and is a sign of their devotion. The lesson explores this phenomenon and the Quranic verse that affirms it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the emphasis on the Ummah's constant practice.
The author uses vivid imagery: land and sea, night and morning.
2
Identify the contrast with other religions
The passage states that people of other religions rarely mention the word of tawhid. This highlights the uniqueness of the Muslim Ummah.
3
Examine the Quranic proof
The author cites Qur'an 48:26 about the word of taqwa. Reflect on how this verse supports the description of the Ummah.
The phrase 'word of taqwa' is often interpreted as kalimat al-ikhlāṣ.
4
Connect to the lesson's theme
Think about how this constant recitation reflects the Ummah's devotion to tawhid and its role as a sign of truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمر أتباعه بالتمسك به وإجراء كلمة الإخلاص على ألسنتهم، فنجدهم على اختلاف طبقاتهم يتنادون بها في البر والبحر والليل والصباح والرواح مصدقين لما وصفوا به في الكتب المنزلة بأنهم يملأون الأرض تهليلاً وتكبيرًا وتحميدًا وتسبيحا، وأهل سائر الأديان لا يذكرونها إلا بالفرط وذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَأَلْزَمَهُمْ كَلِمَةَ التَّقْوَى وَكَانُوا لَحَقِّ بِهَا وَأَهْلَهَا ﴾ ثم الإيمان بصفاته ليسلم عن التشبيه وقد ورد به وأقام الدلالة عليه فيما جاء به من الكتابة نحو قوله: ﴿ وَءَايَةٌ لَهُمُ الَّيْلُ نَسْلَخُ مِنْهُ النَّهَارَ * وإلى قوله: ﴿ذَلِكَ تَقْدِيرُ الْعَزِيزِ الْعَلِيمِ ﴾ وقوله: ﴿ لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ الْبَصِيرُ ﴾ وما ذكر في آخر سورة الحشر.
And He commanded His followers to adhere to it and to utter the word of sincerity (kalimat al-ikhlāṣ) upon their tongues, so we find them, despite their different classes, calling out with it on land and sea, at night and morning and evening, believing what was described about them in the revealed books, that they fill the earth with tahleel, takbeer, tahmeed, and tasbeeh. While the people of other religions only mention it rarely. And that is His saying: {And He obligated them with the word of taqwa, and they were most worthy of it and its people.} Then (there is) iman in His attributes to safeguard from anthropomorphism (tashbīh), and it has been reported and the proof has been established for it in what came in the writing, such as His saying: {And a sign for them is the night, We strip from it the day} and up to His saying: {That is the decree of the Almighty, the Knowing.} And His saying: {There is nothing like unto Him, and He is the Hearing, the Seeing.} And what is mentioned at the end of Surah al-Hashr.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a combined description and proof. It begins by describing the Ummah's constant practice of reciting the word of tawhid (kalimat al-ikhlāṣ) in all circumstances, unlike other religions, and cites Qur'an 48:26 as proof that God obligated them with the word of taqwa. Then it transitions to the necessity of iman in God's attributes (al-īmān bi-ṣifātihi) for the purpose of being safe from tashbīh (anthropomorphism). The author notes that this iman is established by revelation and supported by rational proof, citing Qur'an 36:37-38 (the sign of night and day leading to God's power and knowledge), 42:11 (negating likeness), and the end of Surah al-Hashr (which describes God's names and attributes). The overall structure: first the empirical observation of the Ummah's devotion to tawhid as a sign of its truth, then a doctrinal statement that belief in God's attributes must be affirmed in a way that avoids anthropomorphism, with scriptural evidence.

Key Terms
كلمة الإخلاص
كلمة التقوى
تهليلاً
تكبيرًا
تحميدًا
تسبيحا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The recitation of tawhid is only for scholars or specific times.
Correction: The passage shows that Muslims of all classes recite it everywhere, day and night, as commanded by Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do people of other religions compare to Muslims in mentioning the word of tawhid?
A) They mention it more frequently.
B) They mention it rarely.
C) They mention it only at night.
D) They do not mention it at all.

Key Takeaway
The Muslim Ummah is distinguished by its constant and universal practice of tawhid recitation, as commanded by Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage describes Muslims filling the earth with tahleel, takbeer, tahmeed, and tasbeeh at all times and places. When was the last time your tongue was so occupied with these words that they overflowed into your daily activities? What specific moments today can you mark for deliberate remembrance, and what will you commit to saying aloud?
?
If the Ummah is distinguished by constant tawhid recitation, what does your personal silence or neglect of these phrases say about your connection to this collective identity? Will you allow the noise of the world to drown out the word that defines you, or will you intentionally increase your utterance of it starting now?
?
The verse says Allah obligated them with the word of taqwa and they were most worthy of it. If you were judged today by your worthiness of that word, how would you fare? What concrete step will you take this week to make tawhid more present on your tongue and in your heart?

Iman in Attributes and Avoiding Tashbih
Author states necessity of iman in attributes to avoid tashbih, with Quranic proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that proper belief in Allah's attributes protects against anthropomorphism, supported by Quranic proofs.

Core Idea
Affirming attributes with tanzih is the path to safety.

After discussing tawhid, the author turns to the attributes of Allah. He emphasizes that iman in the attributes must be affirmed in a way that avoids tashbih (likening Allah to creation). The passage provides Quranic evidence for this, including verses that negate likeness and highlight Allah's power and knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the transition from tawhid to attributes.
The phrase 'thumma' indicates a new topic.
2
Identify the purpose of iman in attributes
The passage explicitly states that iman in attributes is 'to safeguard from tashbih'. Understand that affirming attributes correctly prevents likening Allah to creation.
3
Examine the Quranic proofs
The author cites three sets of verses: 36:37-38, 42:11, and the end of Surah al-Hashr. Reflect on how each supports the proper understanding of attributes.
Verse 42:11 directly negates likeness while affirming hearing and seeing.
4
Understand the overall message
The passage teaches that iman in attributes is not only permissible but necessary to avoid error. It is grounded in revelation and reason.

Source Text

What would change in your relationship with Allah if you truly internalized that every natural phenomenon is a direct act of the Almighty, the All-Knowing?

Refutation of Permanence Without a Substrate
Refutes the idea that accidents can have permanence without a substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why permanence cannot exist without a substrate according to the Maturidi kalam tradition.

Core Idea
Permanence is an accident, and accidents require a substrate.

This passage addresses a key objection in kalam: whether accidents can endure without a substrate. The author argues that since permanence is itself an accident, it cannot exist independently. Two rhetorical challenges further demonstrate the incoherence of the opposing view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument.
2
Identify the central claim
The author claims that originated permanence is an accident and thus requires a substrate. Find where he states this.
3
Analyze the two rhetorical challenges
The passage presents two arguments: first, if permanence existed without a substrate, which essence would deserve it? Second, what prevents God from commanding motions and wills to remain? Understand how these strengthen the refutation.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this refutation is important for the broader discussion of divine attributes and the nature of accidents.
It prevents the concept of eternal attributes from being extended to accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تبقى من أجل بقاء لا في محل، وفي تقرر استحالة وجود بقاء غير قائم بمحل ما يبطل كلامه، لأن البقاء الحادث عرض، والعرض لا يوجد بدون محل كما في سائر أنواع الأعراض، وقد بينا قبل هذا ما يوجب بطلان ذلك ويقال له: إذا كان البقاء موجودًا لا في محل فما الذي جعل ذاتاً متعينًا أولى بأن يكون باقيًا به من ذات آخر سواه؟ ولم كان هذا بقاء له دون غيره؟ ويقال له: إنك أطلقت القول باستحالة بقاء الحركات والإرادات فما الذي يمنع الله تعالى من أن يقول لهما: ابقيا ؟ ويطالب بالفرق بين الحركات والإرادات، وبين غيرهما من الأعراض ولن يجد سبيلا إليه
They remain by means of a permanence not in a substrate. And in establishing the impossibility of the existence of a permanence not subsisting in a substrate, what invalidates his statement is that originated permanence is an accident, and an accident does not exist without a substrate, as in the rest of the types of accidents. And we have already explained before this what necessitates the invalidity of that. And it is said to him: If permanence exists not in a substrate, then what made one essence specifically more deserving of being permanent by it than another essence besides it? And why is this permanence for it and not for another? And it is said to him: You have made a general statement about the impossibility of the permanence of motions and wills. So what prevents God most high from saying to them: "Remain"? And he is demanded to differentiate between motions and wills and between other accidents besides them, and he will not find a way to it.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that accidents can have permanence (بقاء) without a substrate (محل). The author argues that originated permanence is an accident (عرض), and accidents cannot exist without a substrate, like all other accidents. He then presents two rhetorical challenges: first, if permanence existed without a substrate, what would make one essence more deserving of being described as permanent by it than another? Second, if the opponent denies the possibility of permanence for motions and wills, what prevents God from commanding them to remain? The opponent is challenged to find a distinction between these accidents and others, but is unable to do so.

Key Terms
بقاء
عرض
محل
جوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think permanence can exist without a substrate if God wills it.
Correction: The author shows that this leads to contradictions, as permanence itself is an accident requiring a substrate.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that permanence cannot exist without a substrate?
A) Because permanence is a substance that does not need a substrate.
B) Because originated permanence is an accident, and accidents cannot exist without a substrate.
C) Because the Quran says so.
D) Because permanence is identical to the essence.

Key Takeaway
Permanence, being an accident, cannot exist without a substrate, refuting the claim of permanence without a substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat your own good deeds or spiritual states as if they could persist without being anchored in a genuine, ongoing relationship with Allah? In what specific area of your life are you relying on a 'floating' permanence rather than building a solid foundation of faith and action?
?
When you see a blessing in your life that seems stable, do you acknowledge that its continuance (baqā') is an accident sustained by Allah every moment, or do you take it for granted as self-sufficient? What would change if you truly believed it could vanish in an instant if Allah did not command it to remain?
?
If Allah can command even motions and wills to remain (as the lesson challenges), why are you hesitant to ask Him to give permanence to your good intentions and actions? What fear or doubt holds you back from making that du'a right now?

The Dilemma Against Karramiyya on Permanence
Refutes Karramiyya on permanence via a logical dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a logical dilemma forces the Karramiyya to either admit absurdity or concede an additional attribute of permanence.

Core Idea
The Karramiyya's claim on permanence collapses under a simple logical dilemma.

In this passage, the author sharpens the debate with the Karramiyya by posing a precise logical dilemma. The question is simple: when Allah commands an essence to 'remain,' does it remain with or without an attribute of permanence? Following the logic reveals a decisive refutation of their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and translation once to get the flow of the argument. Notice how the author sets up a series of conditional questions.
This is a tight logical argument—every clause matters.
2
Identify the two initial options
The first dilemma asks whether the essence remains 'with permanence' or 'without permanence.' Consider the consequences of each answer.
3
Follow the second dilemma
If 'with permanence,' the author asks whether permanence is 'additional' or 'reverting to the essence.' Trace how each branch leads to refutation or postponement.
The branch 'reverting to the essence' is their own view, but refuted later.
4
Reflect on the key reasoning
Notice the argument that if permanence is additional, it would be an accident, and accidents cannot subsist in accidents—this demolishes the Karramiyya position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال للكرامية : إذا قال الله تعالى لذات "ابق". يبقى ذلك الذات ببقاء أم بلا بقاء؟ فإن قالوا : بلا بقاء، أحالوا ، وإن قالوا يبقى ببقاء، قلنا: فالبقاء معنى زائد على الذات، أم راجع إلى الذات؟ فإن قالوا: معنى زائد على الذات فقد انقادوا للحق وظهر بطلان مذهبهم في القول بجواز اتصاف الأعراض بالبقاء لاستحالة قيام الأعراض بالأعراض وإن قالوا: البقاء راجع إلى الذات والباقي باق لذاته لا لمعنى وهو مذهبهم، نبطل عليهم هذا الكلام بعد هذا - إن شاء الله تعالى.
And it is said to the Karramiyya: When God most high says to an essence: "Remain," does that essence remain with permanence or without permanence? If they say: Without permanence, they commit absurdity. And if they say: It remains with permanence, we say: Is the permanence an additional meaning beyond the essence, or does it revert to the essence? If they say: An additional meaning beyond the essence, then they have submitted to the truth and the invalidity of their doctrine regarding the permissibility of accidents being described with permanence becomes apparent, due to the impossibility of accidents subsisting in accidents. But if they say: Permanence reverts to the essence and the permanent is permanent by itself not by a meaning — and that is their doctrine — we shall refute this statement of theirs after this, God willing.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents a dilemma to the Karramiyya regarding God's command 'Remain' to an essence. The author asks whether the essence remains with or without permanence (بقاء). If without permanence, that is absurd because something cannot remain without some attribute of permanence. If with permanence, then the permanence is either an additional meaning (معنى زائد) beyond the essence or it reverts to the essence (راجع إلى الذات). If it is additional, then the Karramiyya's own position that accidents cannot be described with permanence is invalidated because permanence itself is an accident (since it is additional) and would be an accident subsisting in another accident. If it reverts to the essence, then the essence is permanent by itself, which is their own doctrine, but the author promises to refute that later.

Key Terms
بقاء
ذات
معنى زائد
راجع إلى الذات
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's command 'Remain' directly causes permanence without any additional attribute, but the author forces them into a logical dilemma.
Correction: The passage shows that if permanence is not an attribute, then the essence remains without permanence, which is absurd. Thus permanence must be an attribute, either additional or identical to the essence.

Checkpoint
What is the first dilemma posed to the Karramiyya?
A) Whether the essence remains with or without permanence.
B) Whether permanence is a substance or an accident.
C) Whether the essence is eternal or originated.
D) Whether God's command is eternal or created.

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya are forced into a dilemma: either admit absurdity by denying permanence, or concede that permanence is an additional attribute, which contradicts their own doctrine on accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you willing to subject every belief you hold about God to strict logical scrutiny, even if it forces you to abandon a comforting but inconsistent position? What is one belief you have that you have never thought through in this way?
?
The Karramiyya were cornered by a simple dilemma. Have you ever found yourself trapped in a contradiction in your faith? Did you seek to resolve it, or did you simply ignore it? If you ignore it, what does that say about your commitment to truth?
?
This passage shows the power of logical analysis in theology. How deeply have you examined the foundations of your own creed? Will you today take one belief and trace its logical implications, no matter where they lead?

The Main Issue with the Mu'tazila: Is Permanence Additional?
al-Nasafi defines the locus of dispute with the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific point of contention between the Maturidi school and the Mu'tazila regarding the attribute of permanence, and the consensus-based argument that supports the Maturidi position.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila deny that permanence is a distinct attribute; the Maturidis affirm it as an additional meaning.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi clarifies that the central theological dispute with the Mu'tazila is not about whether accidents are bodies or parts, but whether permanence (بقاء) is an additional meaning over and above the essence or simply identical to it. He then reinforces his school's position by appealing to a consensus about how accidents are described.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the author's distinctions and argument sink in.
2
Identify the three debates mentioned
The author mentions debates with al-Nazzam, al-Dirariyya/al-Najjariyya, and the rest of the Mu'tazila. Notice which one is the focus.
The first two are set aside as not the main topic.
3
Focus on the third debate: the main issue
The core dispute is whether permanence is an additional meaning beyond the essence. Compare the two positions: Maturidi says yes, Mu'tazila says it reverts to essence.
4
Understand the consensus argument
The author cites a prior consensus that accidents are described by attributes that revert to the essence (like existence, thingness) not by additional meanings. This supports the Maturidi view that permanence is additional.
This argument shows that permanence, as an accident, should follow the pattern of being described by what reverts to essence—but the author argues it does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكلام مع النظام يقع في كون الألوان والطعوم والروائح والأصوات وغير ذلك أعراضاً أو أجسامًا، وذلك خارج عن غرضنا، وقد ذكرناه قبل هذا. وكذا الكلام مع الضرارية والنجارية يقع في بيان إحالة كون الأعراض أبعاضًا للأجسام، والكلام مع بقية المعتزلة يقع في أن البقاء هل هو معنى زائد على ذات الباقي أم لا؟ عندنا هو معنى زائد على ذات الباقي، وعندهم هو راجع إلى ذاته. هذا هو محل النزاع لتقدم الإجماع: أن الأعراض توصف بما كان راجعا إلى الذات كالوجود والشيئية واللونية والعرضية، ولا توصف بما كان معنى زائدا على الذات
And the discussion with al-Nazzam concerns whether colors, tastes, smells, sounds, and others are accidents or bodies, and that is outside our purpose, and we have mentioned it before this. Likewise, the discussion with al-Dirariyya and al-Najjariyya concerns the explanation of the impossibility of accidents being parts of bodies. And the discussion with the rest of the Mu'tazila concerns whether permanence is an additional meaning beyond the essence of the permanent or not. According to us, it is an additional meaning beyond the essence of the permanent; according to them, it reverts to its essence. This is the locus of dispute, because there is a prior consensus that accidents are described by what reverts to the essence, like existence, thingness, color-ness, and accident-ness, and are not described by what is an additional meaning beyond the essence.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage sets forth the main point of contention with the Mu'tazila, distinguishing it from discussions with other groups. The author notes that debates with al-Nazzam (about colors, etc., being accidents or bodies) and with al-Dirariyya and al-Najjariyya (about accidents being parts of bodies) are not the focus. Rather, the central issue with the rest of the Mu'tazila is whether permanence (بقاء) is an additional meaning (معنى زائد) beyond the essence of the permanent thing or whether it reverts to the essence (راجع إلى ذاته). The author affirms his school's position that it is additional, while the Mu'tazila hold it reverts to the essence. He supports this by citing a prior consensus that accidents are described by attributes that revert to the essence (like existence, thingness, color-ness, accident-ness) and not by attributes that are additional meanings beyond the essence.

Key Terms

معنى زائد
راجع إلى الذات
عرض
وجود
شيئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dispute with the Mu'tazila is about whether accidents are bodies or parts of bodies.
Correction: The author explicitly sets those debates aside; the core dispute is specifically about permanence being an additional meaning or not.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the locus of dispute with the Mu'tazila?
A) Whether colors and tastes are accidents or bodies.
B) Whether accidents can be parts of bodies.
C) Whether permanence is an additional meaning or reverts to the essence.
D) Whether permanence is eternal or created.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi view that permanence is an additional meaning beyond the essence is supported by a consensus that accidents are described by attributes that revert to the essence, not by additional meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider Allah's permanence, do you truly affirm it as a real, distinct attribute, or do you mentally reduce it to a mere synonym for His existence? What would change in your awe of Him if you saw each of His attributes as a distinct reality?
?
The Mu'tazila rejected additional attributes out of fear that it would imply multiplicity in Allah. Is there any truth about Allah that you shy away from because it seems to complicate your understanding? Are you willing to accept that His reality is far more complex than your mind can encompass?
?
If you believed that Allah's permanence is not merely 'Him existing forever' but a distinct quality that He possesses, how would that affect your hope in His mercy and your fear of His justice? Would you rely on His permanence as a source of stability in your life?
