Wisdoms in God's Creation of Disbelief and Sin
Lists three wisdoms behind God's creation of disbelief and sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three rational wisdoms behind God's creation of disbelief and sin, and how they affirm His perfect power, independence, and essential might.

Core Idea
God's creation of opposites displays His perfect power and independence.

A common question about divine decree is why a good God creates evil and disbelief. This passage from Imam al-Nasafi answers with divine wisdoms. The author shows that these wisdoms are not trivial but profound, serving as signs of Allah's perfect power and absolute independence. The following passage enumerates three such wisdoms, each reinforcing the transcendence and self-sufficiency of the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a sense of its rhythm. Then read the translation to understand the literal meaning.
The passage ends mid-sentence in the source; treat it as a complete listing for our lesson.
2
Identify the three wisdoms
The passage explicitly lists three wisdoms (منها). Find each one and note how they are introduced and concluded.
The first wisdom contains an additional sub-point about distinguishing eternal and temporal power.
3
Understand the first wisdom in depth
Focus on how creating opposites (good/ugly, beneficial/harmful) demonstrates perfect power. Contrast with limited power that can only produce one type.
Also note the extra point: this shows God's power over what is action of another, distinguishing His power and will from created ones.
4
Reflect on the second and third wisdoms
The second wisdom shows God's freedom from need; the third shows His independence and might, not dependent on followers or weakened by opposers.
This refutes the misconception that God's creation of evil stems from weakness or need.
5
Connect to the broader creed
These wisdoms affirm divine attributes like power, will, richness (ghani), and might (aziz). They also address the problem of evil from an Islamic theological perspective.
The incomplete third wisdom still conveys the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكمة في خلق الكفر والمعاصي ثم نقول: إن الله تعالى في تخليق الكفر والمعصية حكما لا يحيط بها الإحصاء :منها أن يتخلق ما حسن وقبح من الأفعال ليستدل على كمال قدرته ونفاذ مشيئته حيث قدر على تخليق المتضادين وإيجاد المتقابلين، وهذا آية كمال القدرة إذ من يوجد منه نوع واحد لا غير كان مضطرًا في ذلك، وبهذا كان خلق ما حسن من الأجسام وقبح وطاب وخبث ونفع وضر وآلم وألذ حكمة بالغة وتدبيرا صائبا فكذا هذا، وفي هذا زيادة أمر وهو إظهار القدرة على ما هو فعل غيره، وبه تمتاز القدرة القديمة من القدرة الحادثة والمشيئة الشاملة من المشيئة القاصرة، فيظهر بذلك أنه تعالى قادر على محل قدرة غيره متصرف في مقدور عباده مستبد بتحصيل مراده وغير مفتقر إليه محتاج إلى إعانته وهو الغني الحميد. ومنها : أنه تعالى بتخليقه الأفعال خيرها وشرها حسنها وقبحها تبين أنه ما يفعل لا يفعل عن حاجة ولا لجلب نفع أو لدفع مضرة؛ إذ من ذلك فعله لا يفعل إلا ينتفع به. ومنها: أنه بذلك يظهر أنه تعالى غني عن خلقه عزيز بذاته لا يتعزز بكثرة أوليائه وأتباعه ولا يتقوى بأعوانه وأنصاره، ولا يضعف بتكاتف أحزاب ما
The wisdom in creating disbelief and sins. Then we say: God, exalted, in the creation of disbelief and disobedience has wisdoms that enumeration cannot encompass. Among them: that He creates good and ugly actions so that His perfect power and penetrating will may be inferred, since He is able to create opposites and bring about contraries. This is a sign of perfect power, for one from whom only one type issues is constrained in that. And by this, the creation of good and ugly bodies, and pleasant and foul, and beneficial and harmful, and painful and pleasant, was profound wisdom and sound management. So is this. In this there is an additional matter: the manifestation of power over what is the action of another. By this, the eternal power is distinguished from temporal power, and the comprehensive will from the limited will. Thus it appears that He, exalted, is powerful over the locus of another's power, is dispossessor in the measure of His servants, absolute in achieving His desire, not in need of them, needing their assistance – and He is the Rich, the Praiseworthy. And among them: that He, exalted, by creating actions – their good and evil, their beautiful and foul – shows that what He does, He does not do out of need or to attract benefit or repel harm. From that, His action is not except that He benefits by it. And among them: that by this, it appears that He, exalted, is independent of His creation, mighty in His essence, not made mighty by the multitude of His loyalists and followers, nor strengthened by His helpers and supporters, nor weakened by the coalition of any parties.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a listing of wisdoms (حكم) behind God's creation of disbelief and sin. The author begins by stating that these wisdoms are countless, then enumerates three. The first wisdom: God creates both good and ugly actions so that His perfect power and comprehensive will may be inferred. The ability to create opposites (like good and evil, benefit and harm) is a sign of complete power; one who can only produce one type is constrained. This is analogous to creating opposites in physical bodies. An additional point within this wisdom is that it shows God's power over what is the act of another (i.e., human actions), thereby distinguishing eternal power from temporal power and the comprehensive divine will from limited human will. This demonstrates that God is powerful over even the locus of another's power and is completely independent. The second wisdom: by creating both good and evil actions, God shows that He does not act out of need or for benefit; rather, He is the one who benefits from His own actions (i.e., He is self-sufficient). The third wisdom (incomplete): by this, it appears that God is independent of His creation, mighty in essence, not strengthened by followers nor weakened by opposition.

Key Terms
تخليق
كفر
معصية
قدرة
مشيئة
غني
عزيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's creation of evil implies weakness or need on His part.
Correction: The passage shows that creating opposites demonstrates perfect power, not weakness, and that God's actions are not driven by need but by wisdom. He is independent and mighty in essence.

Checkpoint
What is the first wisdom mentioned for God's creation of disbelief and sin?
A) To test human obedience.
B) To manifest His perfect power over opposites.
C) To punish sinners.
D) To create variety in the world.

Key Takeaway
God's creation of disbelief and sin serves profound wisdoms: showcasing His perfect power over opposites, demonstrating His freedom from need, and affirming His absolute independence and essential might.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see evil or disbelief in the world, do you instinctively question God's wisdom or do you recognize it as a display of His perfect power over opposites? What would it take for you to sincerely affirm that even sin serves a divine purpose?
?
If God is truly independent and does not need your obedience, why do you still feel compelled to obey Him? Does your worship stem from a genuine recognition of His worth or from a desire to benefit yourself?
?
The passage says God's power encompasses your own actions. Do you live as if your choices are entirely under His control? How would your fear of sin and hope in His mercy change if you fully embraced this truth?

God's Wisdom in Creating Kufr and Evil Actions
Imam Nasafi argues that God's creation of kufr is wise, not foolish.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi argues that God's creation of kufr is an act of wisdom, not foolishness.

Core Idea
God's wisdom in creating evil lies in the praiseworthy outcome, not in the evil itself.

This passage from Imam Nasafi addresses a profound theological question: how can a wise and good God create disbelief and evil actions? Nasafi argues that God's creation is wise because it fulfills His knowledge and justice, leading to a praiseworthy outcome. He also distinguishes between the creation of the act and its moral quality, showing that wisdom requires evil to be evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic source passage and its English translation carefully. Let the author's own words sink in before analyzing.
Notice the dense, dialectical style typical of classical kalam.
2
Identify the overall argument
The passage argues that God's creation of kufr is wise. Find the key premise: God knew some would disbelieve, He created that in them at their choice, and this realizes His knowledge and justice.
The 'praiseworthy outcome' is central.
3
Examine the analogy and nuance
Nasafi draws an analogy between creating foul bodies and creating ugly actions. Then he clarifies that we don't say God created kufr absolutely; rather, the creation of kufr as ugly and false is wisdom.
He contrasts the wise Creator with the foolish disbeliever who seeks good from evil.
4
Reflect on the comparison with knowledge
The passage ends with a comparison: knowing something as it truly is (ugly/false) is wisdom, while knowing it as good is ignorance. This reinforces the point that God's wisdom is in creating things according to their true natures.
This helps clarify the difference between God's perspective and the disbeliever's.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها : أنه تعالى لما علم أن بعض عباده يعصونه فيما يأمرهم ويكفرون نعمه، وأنه يعذبهم في النار ويملأ منهم جهنم، كان تعالى بتخليقه ذلك فيهم عند اختيارهم لأنفسهم ذلك، موجدًا ما فيه تحقيق علمه وتقرير عدله عند تعذيبه إياهم عليه، وهذه عاقبة للفعل حميدة، ثم كان إيجاد ما خبث من الأجسام حكمة لما تعلقت بها عاقبة حميدة، فكذا إيجاد ما قبح من الأفعال. على أنا لا نقول على الإطلاق أنه خلق الكفر بل نقول بأن خلق الكفر قبيحًا باطلاً شرا فاسدًا، والحكمة تقتضي كون الكفر على هذه الصفات، فمن أوجده على ما تقتضي الحكمة وجوده عليه كان حكيمًا، وإنما كان سفيها من يقصد تحصيلها حكمةً حسنًا صوابًا كما يقصده الكافر إذ الحكمة تقتضى كونه على ما يضاد هذه الأوصاف فكان الله تعالى بإيجاده على هذه الصفات حكيماً ، والكافر باكتسابه قاصد تلك الصفات سفيها كمن علمه على ما هو عليه من الصفات من القبح والبطلان وغير ذلك كان عالما به، ومن يعلمه على ما يقصده الكافر من الصفات كان جاهلاً به، فكذا في الحكمة والسفه.
And among these: that Allah, when He knew that some of His servants disobey Him in what He commands and disbelieve in His blessings, and that He will punish them in the Fire and fill Hell with them, He - exalted - by creating that in them at the time of their choosing that for themselves, is bringing into existence that which realizes His knowledge and establishes His justice when He punishes them for it. And this is a praiseworthy outcome of the act. Then the bringing into existence of what is foul from bodies was wisdom because a praiseworthy outcome was attached to it, so likewise the bringing into existence of what is ugly from actions. Moreover, we do not absolutely say that He created disbelief, but rather we say that the creation of disbelief is ugly, false, evil, corrupt, and wisdom requires that disbelief be of these attributes. So whoever brings it into existence in the manner that wisdom requires its existence is wise. And only a fool would intend to attain it as wisdom, good, and correct, as the disbeliever intends, because wisdom requires it to be contrary to these attributes. Thus Allah, by bringing it into existence with these attributes, is wise, and the disbeliever by acquiring it intending those attributes is foolish. It is as if one who knows it as it is of the attributes of ugliness and falsity and other than that is knowledgeable of it, and one who knows it as the disbeliever intends of attributes is ignorant of it. So too in the case of wisdom and foolishness.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is an argument that God's creation of kufr and evil acts is an act of wisdom. The author begins with a hypothetical: God knew that some would disobey and disbelieve, and He created that in them at their choice, thereby realizing His knowledge and justice. This outcome is praiseworthy. Then he draws an analogy: just as creating foul bodies can be wise due to a praiseworthy outcome, so is creating ugly actions. He clarifies that they do not say God created kufr absolutely; rather, the creation of kufr as ugly, false, etc. is required by wisdom. The one who brings it into existence as wisdom requires is wise, while the one who intends to acquire it as good is foolish. He compares it to knowledge: knowing something for what it is is knowledge, while knowing it as the opposite is ignorance. So it is with wisdom and foolishness.

Key Terms
الحكمة
السفه
خلق الكفر
عاقبة حميدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's creation of evil means He is evil or foolish.
Correction: The passage clarifies that wisdom lies in the overall outcome (fulfilling knowledge and justice), not in the immediate nature of the act. God creates evil as evil because wisdom requires it to be so, and this is wise.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for calling God wise in creating kufr?
A) Because kufr itself is good
B) Because the outcome fulfills His knowledge and justice
C) Because He does not actually create kufr
D) Because He creates only good acts

Key Takeaway
God's creation of kufr is wise because it fulfills His knowledge and justice, and wisdom requires that evil be evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God's creation of disbelief is wise, how does this affect your attitude toward Muslims who struggle with doubts or toward non-Muslims? Does this lesson challenge any hidden resentment you might hold against God for allowing evil in the world?
?
The passage says the disbeliever is foolish for intending disbelief as good. In what areas of your own life do you foolishly intend something God has declared ugly, thinking it is good for you? Will you repent and seek true wisdom?
?
How does this lesson change how you understand God's justice when you see suffering? Are you willing to accept that God's wisdom may be beyond your comprehension, yet still trust His perfect justice without harboring doubts or complaints?

General vs. Specific Attribution of Creation
Nasafi explains the distinction between general and specific attribution of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between general and specific attribution of creation to Allah, and why Muslims avoid specifically attributing evil acts to Him.

Core Idea
General attribution magnifies Allah; specific attribution honors the thing.

This passage from Imam Nasafi tackles a subtle but important point: how we speak about Allah's creation. While we affirm that Allah creates everything, including human actions and even foul things, we don't say 'Creator of disbelief' specifically. Why? Because the manner of attribution carries meaning—general attribution is a form of praising Allah, while specific attribution is a form of honoring the thing itself. Nasafi explains this using examples like 'House of Allah' and 'Prophet of Allah.' Understanding this nuance protects us from theological errors and helps us speak about Allah with proper reverence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once silently. Then read the English translation. Notice the flow: first a general statement, then the distinction, then examples, then the conclusion.
Pay attention to the key terms 'general attribution' and 'specific attribution.'
2
Identify the two types of attribution
The passage presents two ways of attributing things to Allah: general (كلية) and specific (خاصة). For each type, note its purpose and how it should be used.
General attribution is for praising Allah; specific attribution is for honoring the thing.
3
Reflect on the examples and the rule
The passage gives examples of specific attribution that are permissible (e.g., 'House of Allah') and those that are not (e.g., 'Creator of monkeys and pigs'). Ask yourself: why is one allowed and the other forbidden? The answer lies in the concept of magnification (تعظيم).
Disbelief and evil acts are not magnified, so they cannot be specifically attributed to Allah.

Source Text

ثم نقول: إن الله تعالى خالق كل شيء، ويدخل تحت أفعال الخلق والأجسام الخبيثة وغير ذلك ولا يبالى من ذلك ولكن لا نقول ذلك على التخصيص لما أن إضافة كلية الأشياء إلى الله تعالى وإضافته إلى كلية الأشياء تخرج مخرج التعظيم الله تعالى والتحميد له وإضافة خاصية الأشياء إليه وإضافته إلى خاصية الأشياء تخرج مخرج تعظيم ذلك الخاص كما يقال : إله محمد وإله موسى وإله هارون وعبد الله وبيت الله وناقة الله والكفر والمعاصي ليس بمعظمة فلا يجوز إضافتها إلى الله تعالى على الخصوص، ولهذا لا يجوز أن يقال يا خالق القردة والخنازير، والله أعلم.
Then we say: Indeed, Allah is the Creator of everything, and the actions of creatures and foul bodies and other things enter under His creation, and He does not care about that. But we do not say that specifically because the general attribution of all things to Allah, and His attribution to all things, is expressed in a way of magnifying and praising Allah. And the specific attribution of things to Him, and His attribution to specific things, is expressed as magnifying that specific thing, as it is said: 'God of Muhammad,' 'God of Moses,' 'God of Aaron,' 'Abdullah,' 'House of Allah,' 'She-camel of Allah.' Disbelief and acts of disobedience are not magnified, so it is not permissible to attribute them specifically to Allah. For this reason, it is not permissible to say, 'O Creator of monkeys and pigs.' And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage clarifies the issue of attribution. Allah is the Creator of everything absolutely, including actions and foul bodies. However, specific attribution (e.g., 'Creator of kufr') is avoided because such attribution is a form of honor. The general attribution, like 'Creator of all things,' expresses praise and magnification of Allah. Specific attribution is reserved for things that are honored, like 'House of Allah' or 'Prophet of Allah.' Since disbelief and evil acts are not honored, they are not specifically attributed to Allah. The same applies to monkeys and pigs. This explains why it is inappropriate to say 'O Creator of disbelief.'

Key Terms
الإضافة الكلية (general attribution)
الإضافة الخاصة (specific attribution)
تعظيم (magnification)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The prohibition of specific attribution means that Allah did not create disbelief or evil acts.
Correction: The passage clearly states that Allah creates everything, including foul things and actions. The prohibition is about the manner of speaking: specific attribution honors the thing, so we avoid attributing evil specifically to Allah out of reverence.

Checkpoint
Why is it not permissible to say 'O Creator of disbelief' specifically, according to this passage?
A) Because disbelief is not created by Allah.
B) Because specific attribution is a form of honor, and disbelief is not honored.
C) Because only good things are created by Allah.
D) Because all things are created, but disbelief is a human act.

Key Takeaway
We attribute creation to Allah in general to magnify Him, but avoid specific attribution of evil acts because that would honor what is not honorable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a trial or difficulty, do you ever catch yourself saying 'Allah did this to me' in a specific, personal way? How might the principle in this lesson change the way you speak about such events—and what does that say about your heart's posture towards Allah?
?
Think of a time you referred to someone as 'servant of Allah' ('Abdullah) or 'friend of Allah.' Did you intend to honor that person? Now consider: if you were to specifically attribute an evil act to Allah (e.g., 'Allah created my failure'), would that honor Him or dishonor Him? What does your speech reveal about what you truly magnify?
?
This lesson teaches that words carry the power to magnify or diminish. What phrases do you use most often about Allah? Are they general praises that magnify Him, or do you sometimes use specific attributions that might inadvertently dishonor Him? Commit right now to one change in your speech based on this lesson—write it down and share it with a trusted friend.

Refuting the Claim that Creating Evil Makes God Evil
Nasafi refutes the objection that God would be evil if He created evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi uses consensus and the example of madmen and children to refute the objection that creating evil makes the creator evil.

Core Idea
Creating evil does not make one evil.

The objectors argue that if God created evil, He would be evil. Imam Nasafi responds with two powerful arguments: first, the consensus of rational people that God is not evil, and second, the observable fact that madmen and children commit evil acts without being labeled evil. This lesson unpacks the logic behind this refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the opponent's claim: whoever creates evil is evil, and since God created disbelief (an evil), He would be evil. They also contrast the one who does good vs. the one who creates evil.
Notice how they build a syllogism: Creator of evil is evil; God created evil; therefore God is evil.
2
Identify the two parts of the refutation
First, Nasafi points to the consensus (ijma') of rational people that God is not evil, and that denying this is disbelief. Second, he highlights that madmen and children perform evil acts yet are not called evil. This demolishes the premise that creating evil implies evil character.
These are two independent arguments that together refute the objection.
3
Understand the logical impact
The refutation shows that the causal connection between an act and the agent's moral character is not automatic. The agent's nature and intention matter. Consensus confirms God's perfection, and everyday examples confirm the logical flaw.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: من أوجد الشر فهو شرير، والكفر شر فلو أوجده الله تعالى لكان شريراً، والله تعالى منزه عن هذا الوصف. وربما يقررون هذا ويقولون: الإيمان خير وصلاح والدعاء إليه خير والفاعل للإيمان أصلح من الداعي، والكفر شر والدعاء إليه شر، فيجب أن يكون خالق الكفر شر وأفسد من هذا الداعي. والجواب عنه: أنا قد أقمنا الدلالة على أن الله تعالى هو المخترع لكل حادث ولا قدرة لغيره على الاختراع، وانعقد إجماع العقلاء أن الله تعالى ليس بشرير، ومن زعم أنه شرير فهو كافر فظهر من مجموع الدليلين أن موجد ما هو شر ليس بشرير. والذي يهدم عليهم هذه القاعدة أنهم يزعمون أن ما هو الكفر والظلم والقبح مما يصح من المجانين والأطفال ويكون ذلك شرا بالوجود، ولا يصح أن يوصف واحد منهم بأنه شرير.
If they say: Whoever brings evil into existence is evil. Disbelief is evil, so if Allah brought it into existence, He would be evil, and Allah is exalted above this description. Sometimes they formulate this and say: Faith is good and righteousness, and calling to it is good, and the doer of faith is more righteous than the caller. Disbelief is evil and calling to it is evil, so the creator of disbelief must be more evil and more corrupt than this caller. The answer to this is: We have established the proof that Allah is the creator of every occurring thing, and no one else has the power to create. And the consensus of rational people is that Allah is not evil, and whoever claims He is evil is a disbeliever. So from both proofs it appears that the one who brings into existence that which is evil is not evil. And what demolishes this rule for them is that they claim that disbelief, injustice, and ugliness are things that occur from madmen and children, and that is evil in existence, yet none of them is described as evil.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection that if God creates evil, He would be evil. The objectors argue that the creator of evil is worse than the one who calls to evil. The response is twofold: first, the author has already proven that God is the sole creator, and it is a consensus that God is not evil; therefore, creating evil does not necessitate being evil. Second, they point out that evil acts like disbelief and injustice occur from madmen and children, yet we do not call them evil. This shows that the occurrence of evil from someone does not make that person evil. The argument refutes the objectors' premise.

Key Terms
شرير (evil person)
المجانين والأطفال (madmen and children)
إجماع العقلاء (consensus of the rational)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any agent who brings evil into existence must be evil themselves.
Correction: The passage shows that even madmen and children produce evil acts without being labeled evil, and the consensus of rational people affirms God is not evil despite being the creator of all things.

Checkpoint
What example does Imam Nasafi use to show that bringing evil into existence does not make one evil?
A) Prophets who commit sins
B) Animals that cause harm
C) Madmen and children who commit evil acts
D) Angels who create evil

Key Takeaway
Creating evil does not make one evil, as proven by consensus and the example of madmen and children.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God’s permission of evil in your life makes Him less worthy of your love and trust? What would change if you truly internalized that creating evil does not make Him evil?
?
When you see suffering or injustice in the world, do you secretly question God’s goodness? This lesson argues that even the worst evils are created by God yet He is not evil. Can you accept that judgment even when it feels emotionally impossible? What would it take to surrender that doubt to Him tonight?
?
If you truly believe God is not evil despite creating evil, then what obligation do you have to trust Him in your personal trials? Think of one specific trial you are facing right now. Are you willing to say, 'My Creator is not evil, so this trial must have a purpose'? If not, what stops you?

On Not Specifying Vile Creations to Allah
The author answers an objection about God creating vile things by explaining the rule of attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between general and specific attribution of creation to Allah, and why vile things are not specifically attributed to Him.

Core Idea
Specific attribution magnifies the thing; vile things are not magnified, so we avoid specifying them.

An objector argues that if God created everything, He must be directly associated with vile creations like pigs and beetles. The author answers by explaining a key rule: general attribution is praise, but specific attribution magnifies the specific thing. Since vile things are not honorable, we do not attribute them specifically to Allah. This passage clarifies a subtle point of divine attribution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its full translation to get a sense of the argument's flow.
Notice how the author first addresses an objection before stating his own position.
2
Identify the objection and the response
Find where the objectors' challenge is presented and where the author gives his first logical counter.
The objection is implied: 'If God created everything, then He created vile things specifically.'
3
Understand the rule of attribution
Focus on the distinction between general attribution (praise) and specific attribution (magnification of the thing).
The examples of 'God of Muhammad' and 'House of Allah' illustrate honorable specific attribution.
4
Apply the rule to vile things
Understand why disbelief and sins are not specifically attributed to Allah even though He creates all actions.
The conclusion is that we do not say 'O Creator of monkeys' because that would honor monkeys.
5
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the quiz question to confirm you grasp the key reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخنازير والخنافس والجعلان والأقذار والأنتان، وحيث لم يجز دل أنه لم يخلقها، فإن كان ما سألتم لازمًا فهذا لازم، وإن كان ما سألوا باطلا فسؤالكم باطل. ثم نقول: إن الله تعالى خالق كل شيء، ويدخل تحت أفعال الخلق والأجسام الخبيثة وغير ذلك ولا يبالى من ذلك ولكن لا نقول ذلك على التخصيص لما أن إضافة كلية الأشياء إلى الله تعالى وإضافته إلى كلية الأشياء تخرج مخرج التعظيم الله تعالى والتحميد له وإضافة خاصية الأشياء إليه وإضافته إلى خاصية الأشياء تخرج مخرج تعظيم ذلك الخاص كما يقال: إله محمد وإله موسى وإله هارون وعبد الله وبيت الله وناقة الله والكفر والمعاصي ليس بمعظمة فلا يجوز إضافتها إلى الله تعالى على الخصوص، ولهذا لا يجوز أن يقال يا خالق القردة والخنازير، والله أعلم.
And the pigs, and the beetles, and the dung beetles, and the filths, and the stinks. And since it is not permissible, it indicates that He did not create them. If what you asked is necessary, then this is necessary; and if what they asked is false, then your question is false. Then we say: Indeed, Allah, exalted, is the Creator of everything, and the actions of creatures and vile bodies and other things fall under that, and He does not care about that. But we do not say that with specification, because the attribution of all things to Allah, exalted, and His attribution to all things, comes in the context of magnification and praise of Allah, exalted; and the attribution of a specific thing to Him and His attribution to a specific thing comes in the context of magnifying that specific thing—as it is said: 'The God of Muhammad,' 'The God of Moses,' 'The God of Aaron,' 'Abdullah (servant of Allah),' 'The House of Allah,' 'The She-camel of Allah.' Disbelief and sins are not magnified, so it is not permissible to attribute them specifically to Allah, exalted. For this reason, it is not permissible to say: 'O Creator of monkeys and pigs.' And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The objectors argued that if God created everything, then He must have created specific vile things like pigs and beetles, implying that such creations are directly from Him. The author responds by first stating that the impermissibility of such an attribution indicates that God did not create them in that specific sense. He then explains that while God is the Creator of all things without exception, including vile bodies, we do not specify such things when attributing creation to God. The reason is that general attribution (e.g., 'Creator of everything') is a form of praise and magnification, whereas specific attribution (e.g., 'Creator of pigs') would magnify that specific thing. Since disbelief and sins are not objects of veneration, they should not be specifically attributed to God. The author gives examples: we say 'the God of Muhammad' to honor Muhammad, but we do not say 'the Creator of pigs' because that would honor pigs. Thus, the rule is established: God creates all things, but we only specify good and noble things when attributing creation to Him.

Key Terms
خالق
إضافة
تعظيم
تحميد
خاص
على الخصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's creation of evil implies He is directly associated with evil in a demeaning way.
Correction: The passage clarifies that while God creates all things, we do not specifically attribute vile creations to Him because that would magnify them. General attribution is praise for God, not an association with evil.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it not permissible to say 'O Creator of monkeys and pigs'?
A) Because God did not create them.
B) Because such a specific attribution would magnify those creatures.
C) Because the Qur'an forbids mentioning animals.
D) Because only prophets can be specifically attributed to God.

Key Takeaway
Allah creates all things, but we only specify noble and good things when attributing creation to Him, because specific attribution magnifies the thing attributed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about Allah's creation, do you focus on the negative or vile aspects of the world in a way that might dishonor Him? How can you train yourself to only specify what is noble when mentioning Allah's works?

Are there people in your life whom you honor by associating them with Allah in your speech (e.g., 'servant of Allah')? Are there those whom you dishonor by your speech? What does this reveal about your heart?
?
If you truly believe Allah creates all things, including the difficult trials in your life, do you ever accuse Him of creating evil in you? How does this principle of not specifying evil to Allah change how you should talk about your own sins and struggles?

The Creation of Evil and the Meaning of 'Ahsana Kulla Shay'in Khalaqahu'
The author refutes the objection that God cannot create evil using the verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse 'Who made everything He created good' does not exclude evil acts from being created by God, and the proper interpretation of the verse.

Core Idea
God creates both good and evil; the verse means He created everything with perfect wisdom.

This lesson addresses a common objection against divine creation of evil based on Quran 32:7. The author clarifies the verse's true meaning and refutes the objection using examples from creation. By the end, you will see why the verse does not imply that Allah only creates beautiful things.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the verse
Start by reading the objection: how can evil be created by God when He says He made everything good? This is the core challenge.
Notice the verse is Quran 32:7.
2
Understand the author's interpretation
The author explains that 'ahsana' means He created well, knowing the qualities of what He creates, both beautiful and ugly.
The focus is on the perfection of creation, not the aesthetic quality.
3
Examine the analogy with human actions
The author compares divine creation to human actions: we say someone does something well when they achieve their intended outcome with knowledge.
Examples include writing, painting, carpentry, and even killing.
4
Consider the examples from creation
Beetles, dung beetles, monkeys, and pigs are created by God. If disbelief were excluded from His creation, these would also be excluded, which is false.
This shows the consistency of God's creative act.
5
Recognize the final argument
Denying the ugliness of these creatures is a denial of necessity (what is rationally known). Such denial leads to absurdity, like denying the ugliness of disbelief.
Thus, the objection is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: كيف يجرؤ أن يكون القبيح خلق الله تعالى والله تعالى يقول: ﴿ الَّذِي أَحْسَنَ كُلَّ شَيْءٍ خَلَقَهُ ﴾. قلنا: معنى ذلك أنه أحسن خلق الأشياء لأنه عالم بكيفية خلقها على ما هي عليه من القبح والحسن فكانت على ما أراده ولم تكن على خلاف ذلك، ومن فعل شيء فكان على ما قصده وكان عالما بتحصيله وتحصيله على ما أراد، يقال : فلان يحسن فعل كذا وفلان يحسن الكتابة والصباغة والنجارة وفلان يحسن القتل، وما يوضح ذلك أنه تعالى خلق الخنافس والجعلان والقردة والخنازير، فلو خرج الكفر عن خلقه بقضية هذه الآية لخرجت هذه الأشياء وذلك باطل، فكذا هذا. ولا وجه لإنكارهم قبح هذه الأشياء كما لا وجه لإنكار حسن كثير من الأجسام؛ لأنه إنكار الضرورة ولو جاز له هذا جاز لغيره إنكار قبح الكفر، وذلك باطل.
For they say: How can it be that the ugly (al-qabīḥ) is the creation of God Almighty while God Almighty says: 'Who made everything He created good'? We say: The meaning of that is that He made the creation of things good because He knows the manner of their creation according to what they are upon of ugliness and beauty, so they were as He intended and were not contrary to that. And when someone does something according to what he intended and was knowledgeable of its attainment and its attainment was as he intended, it is said: so-and-so does such-and-such well, and so-and-so writes well, paints well, carpenters well, and so-and-so kills well. What clarifies that is that He created beetles, dung beetles, monkeys, and pigs. If disbelief were excluded from His creation by the purport of this verse, these things would be excluded, and that is false. So likewise this. And there is no basis for their denial of the ugliness of these things, just as there is no basis for denying the beauty of many bodies; because that is a denial of necessity. And if this were permissible for them, it would be permissible for others to deny the ugliness of disbelief, and that is false.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer passage. The opponents object that it is impossible for evil acts to be created by God because God says 'Who made everything He created good' (Quran 32:7). The author responds that the meaning of the verse is that God perfected the creation of things, because He knows the manner of their creation according to their qualities—both ugly and beautiful—and thus they came into existence as He intended, not contrary to that. The author further explains that when someone does something according to his intent and knowledge, it is said that he does that thing well; for example, one person does writing well, another painting, carpentry, and even killing. To clarify, God created beetles, dung beetles, monkeys, and pigs. If disbelief were excluded from His creation by the apparent meaning of this verse, these things would also be excluded, which is false. So likewise, disbelief is not excluded. Then he adds that there is no basis for the objectors to deny the ugliness of these creatures, just as there is no basis to deny the beauty of many bodies, because that would be a denial of necessity. If they were allowed to deny the ugliness of these, others would be allowed to deny the ugliness of disbelief, which is false.

Key Terms
القبيح
خلق الله
أحسن كل شيء خلقه
القبح والحسن
إنكار الضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse means everything created is physically beautiful.
Correction: The author clarifies it means He created everything with perfect wisdom, including ugly things.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the verse 'الَّذِي أَحْسَنَ كُلَّ شَيْءٍ خَلَقَهُ' mean?
A) God created only beautiful things.
B) God created everything with knowledge of its qualities, both good and evil.
C) God created everything as good.
D) The verse excludes ugly things.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Who made everything He created good' does not negate the creation of evil; rather, it affirms that Allah creates everything with perfect knowledge and wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face suffering or witness evil, do you subconsciously assume that God could not have willed it because He is 'good'? How does this lesson's reinterpretation of 'ahsana' challenge that assumption and change your perspective on trials in your life?
?
The author says that even killing can be done 'well' if it is with knowledge and intent. Is there an area of your life where you resist God's will because it appears 'ugly' to you—like a difficult relationship, a loss, or a personal weakness? Are you willing to trust that God created that situation with perfect wisdom even if you cannot see its beauty now?
?
If the verse does not mean everything God creates is physically or morally beautiful, then your standard for judging God's actions might be flawed. Will you commit to stop measuring God's goodness by your own limited sense of what is 'good' and instead accept His wisdom as absolute, even when it brings pain?
